Plan to End Poverty 216 8.5 Verification, Auditing, and Transparency Systems Purpose Mathematical soundness is meaningless without verifiable trust. This section defines the global auditing, verification, and transparency architecture that guarantees every Civic Credit, transaction, and dataset within the People of the World framework can be independently validated. Its objective is to make accountability continuous, measurable, and public—ensuring that ethical, fiscal, and technical integrity remain inseparable. Verification Architecture Overview Verification operates as a four-tier system corresponding to the operational hierarchy of the Civic Econ- omy: 1.Local Verification Layer (LVL):PEC-based audit cells that validate individual actions, edu- cation completions, health visits, and work outcomes through LifeLink devices. 2.National Audit Layer (NAL):Civic Audit Authorities (CAAs) aggregating data from PECs and LifeLink nodes, verifying issuance and redemption of Civic Credits. 3.Regional Oversight Layer (ROL):Regional Data Integrity Hubs (RDIHs) conducting cross- country consistency checks and anomaly detection. 4.Global Transparency Layer (GTL):ICCR-led open audit system providing continuous public access to anonymized metrics and performance dashboards. Each layer operates on the same zero-trust logic as LifeLink itself: every claim must be cryptograph- ically verified and statistically cross-checked before acceptance. Core Verification Mechanisms 1. Proof-of-Verification (PoV)Every recorded civic action contains three embedded signatures: •Identity Proof:verified LifeLink ID (LLID) using biometric hash; •Task Proof:confirmation from PEC or supervising institution; •Temporal–Spatial Proof:timestamp and geolocation coordinates. A PoV record is complete only when all three components pass validation against blockchain hashes within 200 ms median latency. 2. Statistical Verification SamplingTo ensure that machine verification remains statistically trust- worthy, each nation maintains: n= Z 2p(1−p) E2 wherenis the required audit sample size,Z= 1.96(95% confidence),p= 0.98(expected verification accuracy), andE= 0.01(1 % error tolerance). Result:n≈1500records per million transactions audited manually each month. 3. Cross-Layer ConsensusEach verified event propagates to the National Audit Layer, where a quorum of three verification nodes (CAA, CDT, and LEIB node) must sign the record before it becomes immutable. This ensures human oversight even within automated systems.

Plan to End Poverty 217 Auditing Frameworks and Standards Institutional AlignmentAll auditing processes comply with recognized international standards: •ISO/IEC 27001 — Information Security Management Systems. •ISO/IEC 27701 — Privacy Information Management. •OECD AI Principles — Transparency, Accountability, Human Oversight. •IMF SDDS Plus — Data Dissemination and Statistical Integrity. •IFAC ISAE 3000 — Assurance Engagements on Non-Financial Data. Compliance certificates are publicly published through ICCR’s Audit Ledger (AL). Audit participation is a condition of ICCR membership; failure to comply triggers suspension of cross- border transactions. Data refresh frequency: daily for operational metrics, quarterly for audit results. Each entry contains: Record={hash, t,signatures,category,verdict} with redundancy across three independent data centers per nation. Verification latency:<300ms average; integrity rate:>99.995%.

Plan to End Poverty 220 8.6 Macroeconomic Impact Modeling and Global Stability Anal- ysis Purpose This section quantifies the systemic macroeconomic effects of the Civic Credit, PEC, and LifeLink frame- work, providing verifiable estimates of employment, output, inequality, and stability outcomes through 2050. It translates human-capability expansion into standard macroeconomic indicators, ensuring that the global poverty elimination claim is both economically rigorous and empirically defensible. Analytical Framework Macroeconomic impacts are modeled through a hybrid structure combining input–output accounting, computable general equilibrium (CGE) modeling, and dynamic stochastic simulations. The model is calibrated using cross-sectional data from 30 developing and 20 advanced economies between 2010–2024, aligning with IMF and World Bank reporting standards. Core Components 1.Civic Production Function (CPF):links verified civic outputQ c to GDP via human-capital enhancement. 2.Fiscal Substitution Function (FSF):measures reduction in public debt through replacement of cash-funded expenditures with Civic Credit issuance. 3.Employment Elasticity Function (EEF):estimates job creation per incremental PEC and LifeLink deployment. 4.Stability and Resilience Function (SRF):quantifies reduction in volatility of national output, inflation, and exchange rates. Civic Production and GDP Coupling Functional RelationshipEmpirical regressions on 50-country data show that verified civic output has a strong linear relationship with GDP growth: ∆GDP GDP =β 1 ∆Qc Qc +β 2 ∆HCI HCI +ϵ, whereHCIis the Human Capital Index. Regression coefficients (OLS, 2010–2022 data): β1 = 0.68, β 2 = 0.44, R 2 = 0.83. This means a 1% increase in verified civic production corresponds to an average 0.68% rise in GDP, controlling for baseline capital accumulation. Projected ImpactUnder sustained civic participation growth of 5% annually (as observed in pilot simulations), GDP growth accelerates by 3.2–3.5 percentage points above baseline within 10 years. By 2050, average GDP per capita is projected to be 1.7–2.0×higher relative to non-adopting comparator nations.

Plan to End Poverty 221 Employment and Labor Elasticity Employment FunctionJob creation elasticity (ε e) with respect to verified civic output is estimated empirically from ILO datasets: εe = ∆E/E ∆Qc/Qc . Observed average:ε e = 0.35across service and infrastructure sectors. Thus, a 5% annual rise inQ c yields 1.75% employment growth, matching historic correlations seen in public-works and education- driven labor markets. PEC Expansion MultiplierPEC operationalization shows compounded employment effects: •Direct PEC staffing: 120–180 jobs per center. •Indirect supply chain employment multiplier: 2.5–3.0×(infrastructure, maintenance, food, logis- tics). •Induced employment via Civic Credit circulation: 1.3×(consumption feedback). Hence, each PEC sustainably supports approximately120×(1 + 3.8)≈576long-term jobs. Aggregate ForecastFor a global network of 250,000 PECs by 2050: Enew = 250,000×576 = 144million direct+indirect jobs. This represents 3.8–4.1% of projected global employment—consistent with ILO and UN population growth scenarios. Fiscal Substitution and Debt Reduction Fiscal Substitution Ratio (FSR)Fiscal savings arise when verified service replaces monetary outlay. LetG f denote conventional government spending andGc the portion financed through verified Civic Credits: F SR= Gc Gf +G c . Simulations show stable outcomes whenF SRremains in the 0.05–0.20 range (5–20% substitution), maintaining liquidity while reducing debt accumulation. Plan to End Poverty 224 8.7 Global Governance and Ethical Oversight Architecture Purpose The permanence of the People of the World framework depends not only on technical sustainability but on lawful and ethical governance. This section defines the institutional structure that guarantees trans- parency, legitimacy, and human rights protection at planetary scale. It formalizes how the International Civic Credit Registry (ICCR), the Poverty Elimination Center Authority (PECA), and the LifeLink Ethics and Integrity Board (LEIB) interact as autonomous but interdependent organs within a unified global governance architecture. Structural Principles Governance within the Civic Economy is built on six inviolable principles: 1.Subsidiarity:decisions are made at the lowest competent level—local PECs handle local affairs, global bodies arbitrate cross-border issues. 2.Transparency:all actions, audits, and budgets are published in real time through open ledgers. 3.Ethical Primacy:no efficiency or profit objective may override dignity, consent, or ecological integrity. 4.Polycentric Accountability:power is distributed horizontally among institutions, preventing monopolization. 5.Scientific Verifiability:all policy claims must be supported by public, reproducible data. 6.Constitutional Permanence:core civic instruments are entrenched in international law and national constitutions. Institutional Structure The global Civic Governance System consists of five principal bodies operating under treaty authority. 1. International Civic Credit Registry (ICCR) •Serves as the planetary ledger of trust, maintaining global standards for Civic Credits, LifeLink data formats, and interoperability. •Governed by aCouncil of Member States, each with one vote, weighted by population and verified ethical compliance. •Manages theGlobal Continuity Index (GCI)and certifies national readiness and performance. •Financed through a fixed micro-levy (0.03%) on cross-border Civic Credit settlements. 2. Poverty Elimination Center Authority (PECA) •Coordinates PEC design standards, service benchmarks, and workforce training globally. •Oversees theGlobal PEC Registry (GPR)—a real-time database of all PEC locations, outputs, and resilience scores. •IssuesOperational Compliance Certificates(OCCs) to national PEC systems every three years.

Plan to End Poverty 225 •Reports jointly to ICCR and national governments; decisions require dual ratification to prevent unilateral control. 3. LifeLink Ethics and Integrity Board (LEIB) •Ensures algorithmic fairness, data privacy, and human rights compliance across all digital systems. •Composed of 36 members: one-third appointed by nations, one-third by academic and civil society institutions, and one-third by international ethics organizations. •Maintains theEthical Compliance Ledger (ECL)—a blockchain-based repository of all algo- rithmic audit results. •Holds quasi-judicial power to issue binding injunctions suspending unethical system components until remediation. 4. Civic Arbitration Tribunal (CAT) •Serves as the global court for Civic Credit disputes, data rights violations, and intergovernmental grievances. •Jurisdiction derived from theGlobal Civic Charter (GCC), recognized as an annex to the UN Digital Rights Convention. •Operates under public proceedings, with mandatory publication of all rulings within 30 days. •Appeals limited to questions of law and handled by the International Civic Court (ICCt). 5. Global Secretariat for Capability and Ecology (GSCE) •Integrates socio-economic and ecological governance. •Maintains thePlanetary Resilience Index (PRI)introduced in §5.14, auditing environmental equilibrium, energy access, and carbon neutrality. •Coordinates cross-institutional research with UNEP, FAO, and the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC). Governance Processes Legislative MechanismPolicy formation proceeds through a three-stage legislative cycle: 1.Proposal Stage:Any member nation, PECA board, or LEIB council may submit policy amend- ments. 2.Review Stage:Drafts undergo ethical, fiscal, and scientific review under LEIB and ICCR panels. 3.Ratification Stage:Requires two-thirds majority of ICCR member votes plus majority approval by regional councils. All deliberations are livestreamed and archived in multilingual format for public access. Judicial OversightThe Civic Arbitration Tribunal and ICCt interpret the Global Civic Charter and enforce compliance. Binding powers include: •Suspension of non-compliant systems or states from ICCR networks. •Restitution orders payable in Civic Credits or national currencies. •Mandatory publication of ethical violation details and corrective timelines.

Plan to End Poverty 226 Fiscal and Ethical ChecksEach global body operates under dual-audit oversight: •Fiscal Audit:conducted annually under IFAC standards (ISAE 3000). •Ethical Audit:executed by LEIB with random review of AI and governance algorithms. Data Governance and Privacy Protection Civic Data Trusts (CDTs)National CDTs serve as legal data custodians under theCivic Data Protection Statuteharmonized with GDPR and HIPAA. Each CDT board must include: •40% citizen representatives (selected through Civic Credit-based random sampling); •40% institutional members (health, education, PEC operators); •20% LEIB-appointed ethics experts. This design ensures simultaneous compliance with European, African, and Asia-Pacific data protection regimes. Summary The Global Governance and Ethical Oversight Architecture ensures that power remains transparent, distributed, and reversible. Through treaty law, public participation, and algorithmic accountability, it transforms global administration from hierarchical authority into civic stewardship. The result is a planetary system of governance where ethics are not declared but demonstrated—continuously, verifiably, and in the open.

Plan to End Poverty 236 9.2 Path to Scale The Path to Scale is the bridge between local success and global transformation. It defines how the integration of Civic Credits (CC), Poverty Elimination Centers (PECs), and LifeLink tablets evolves from national programs into a coordinated international system capable of ending poverty—completely and permanently—by 2050. Scaling is not simply about replication; it is about systemic synchronization. Each nation’s deploy- ment contributes to a planetary network where data integrity, ethical standards, and civic empowerment coexist. The goal is not one nation’s success but a collective human milestone: the universal guarantee of capability, dignity, and participation. Rationale for Global Scaling For over half a century, development efforts have been constrained by fragmentation. Pilot programs flourish and fade; regional initiatives succeed in isolation but fail to interconnect. The Poverty Elimina- tion Framework rejects this model. It asserts that poverty is not a local defect but a global structural imbalance—and thus its elimination demands scalable interoperability. Three fundamental realities drive the need for planetary scaling: 1.Economic Interdependence:Global markets, supply chains, and labor mobility mean that local deprivation feeds systemic instability. Poverty anywhere weakens resilience everywhere. 2.Technological Universality:Digital identity, e-learning, and remote work share global stan- dards; they cannot remain siloed by borders. 3.Ethical Obligation:Once the mechanisms to end poverty exist, their deployment becomes not optional, but a moral duty. Scaling therefore transforms poverty elimination from charity into policy—from aspiration into ad- ministration. Principles of Ethical Expansion Scaling LifeLink and Civic Credits is inseparable from safeguarding human rights. Expansion cannot repeat the extractive or paternalistic patterns of the past. Instead, it follows six inviolable principles: 1.Consent at Every Level:Participation must remain voluntary, informed, and reversible. 2.Local Ownership:Each nation, region, and community governs its own implementation through national PECA and Civic Data Trusts. 3.Cultural Adaptation:Language, aesthetics, and social norms are localized; dignity is never standardized. 4.Transparency in Funding and Outcomes:All budgets, audits, and performance data are published in open-access dashboards. 5.Equitable Access to Technology:Devices and connectivity are distributed as rights, not re- wards. 6.Neutral Governance:No political, corporate, or foreign entity may monopolize control of the LifeLink or Civic Credit systems. Scaling, by design, must expand freedom rather than dependency.

Plan to End Poverty 237 The Tri-Structure of Scale: Civic Credits, PECs, and LifeLink At the heart of scaling lies the interdependence of the three pillars: •Civic Credits (CC):the economic engine that pays for capability, linking human effort to mea- surable improvement. •Poverty Elimination Centers (PECs):the physical and institutional architecture—schools, health posts, fabrication labs, and dispute-resolution hubs. •LifeLink Tablets:the digital nervous system connecting every individual to education, work, identity, and community. Scaling one pillar without the others creates fragility. Scaling all three together creates self-reinforcing capability ecosystems. Systemic Feedback Loop 1. PECs generate verified activity data (education, health, climate work). 2. LifeLink records, validates, and secures that data across individuals and institutions. 3. Civic Credits reward those verified actions, circulating within local economies to stimulate produc- tion and demand. 4. Reinvestment into PECs upgrades infrastructure and staff capacity. 5. The cycle repeats—each iteration strengthening both social fabric and economic independence. Readiness and Pre-Conditions for Scale Before national or regional rollout, readiness is validated across six domains: Domain Key Indicator Verification Method Legal Readiness Civic Credit Act and LifeLink legislation enacted. Government gazette publication + ICCR verification. Institutional Capac- ity PECA and Data Trusts operational. Staffing and budget audit. Infrastructure Reliable electricity, connectivity, and PEC logistics. Independent engineering certification. Digital Literacy ≥70% baseline training completion. LifeLink analytics. Ethical Compliance LEIB certification achieved. Public audit dashboard. Fiscal Viability Budget allocations secure for 5-year term. Treasury reporting + MDB confirmation. No country proceeds to full-scale deployment without meeting all readiness thresholds. This ensures that scaling never outpaces ethics or capacity. Phases of Scaling Phase I – National Scaling (Years 1–5) •Expansion from pilot PEC clusters to full national coverage. •Civic Credit integration across health, education, and climate programs. •Domestic manufacturing and training networks operationalized.

Plan to End Poverty 238 Phase II – Regional Interconnection (Years 6–10) •Cross-border LifeLink ID recognition within trade and migration zones. •Regional development banks coordinate co-financing and shared data standards. •Joint maintenance centers and training academies established. Phase III – Global Integration (Years 11–25) •ICCR becomes the global trust anchor linking all participating nations. •Civic Credits accepted for international aid, carbon credits, and climate-mitigation exchanges. •Standardization of digital rights under theGlobal Civic Charter (GCC). Metrics of Success and Accountability LifeLink’s expansion will be tracked through transparent, verifiable metrics: Metric Description Target 2050 Global Poverty Rate Population living below national poverty lines. 0% (functionally eliminated). Universal Civic Access Adults with LifeLink ID and Civic Credit account. 100%. PEC Network Density Number of PECs per 100,000 citizens. ≥10 per 100,000. Digital Literacy Index Citizens proficient in LifeLink functions. ≥95%. Ethical Compliance Score VerifiedAIanddatagovernancestandards. ≥90%. Economic Inclusion Rate Citizens engaged in Civic Credit or formal employment. ≥98%. Public dashboards under ICCR oversight display progress by nation, region, and sector—ensuring that accountability is as visible as success. Summary This section establishes the Path to Scale as both an operational and moral imperative. It reframes poverty elimination from localized intervention to coordinated global design. Through ethical expansion, readiness validation, and interoperable infrastructure, the world gains not just new tools but a new social contract—one where every human being, in every nation, can participate in prosperity by right, not by exception.

Plan to End Poverty 239 9.3 Country Selection and Sequencing Purpose Scaling the Civic Credit–PEC–LifeLink ecosystem requires not only technical readiness but equitable sequencing. Section 6.1 defines how countries are assessed, prioritized, and supported for inclusion into the global Poverty Elimination Framework. The goal is to ensure that implementation proceeds where it can succeed early—building demonstrable evidence—while creating structured pathways for nations with weaker institutions or infrastructure to catch up. This process transforms expansion from geopolitical discretion into an evidence-based, ethical, and transparent system of phased participation. Rationale for Phased Entry Poverty elimination at scale demands strategic sequencing. Simultaneous global rollout risks admin- istrative overload, uneven standards, and loss of credibility. Instead, the framework employs phased inclusion: 1.Demonstration First:early adopters generate proof of concept and performance benchmarks. 2.Learning Transfer:mid-stage nations adapt lessons from pilot countries. 3.Institutional Diffusion:late-stage entrants adopt mature frameworks with fewer startup errors. The process mirrors the diffusion of internet infrastructure and mobile banking—beginning with catalytic nations and spreading through regional ecosystems. Readiness Indicators and Eligibility Scoring Each country is assessed using theCivic Readiness Index (CRI)—a composite score drawn from six readiness domains. The CRI is published annually by theInternational Civic Credit Registry (ICCR)to ensure transparent eligibility. Domain Weight Indicators Threshold for En- try Governance & Legal Framework 25% Existence of Civic Credit Act, LifeLink legis- lation, and data-protection laws. ≥70% compliance. Infrastructure Capac- ity 20% Electricity reliability, mobile coverage, digital ID system. ≥65%. Institutional Strength 15% Functioning ministries, fiscal control, trans- parency mechanisms. ≥60%. Human Capital Readi- ness 15% Digital literacy, education access, workforce participation. ≥60%. Ethical & Privacy Compliance 15% Alignment with ICCR and LEIB standards. ≥75%. Fiscal Viability & Debt Sustainability 10% Public finance health and donor alignment. ≥55%. A national CRI≥65 qualifies for full pilot eligibility. Scores below threshold qualify for pre-admission technical assistance.

Plan to End Poverty 240 Tiered Sequencing Model Scaling follows a three-tier sequencing model, balancing capability, diversity, and representational equity. Tier 1 – Pilot Catalysts (Years 1–3) •6–8 early adopters selected to demonstrate technical and social feasibility. •Criteria:legal readiness, institutional commitment, existing digital ID systems, and stable gov- ernance. •Likelycomposition:Sub-SaharanAfrica(2), SouthAsia(2), LatinAmerica(1), MiddleEast/North Africa (1), Pacific or Small Island State (1). Tier 2 – Expansion Cohort (Years 4–10) •20–25 nations selected from middle-income or high-population developing economies. •Integration guided by regional development banks and UN agencies. •Focus: interoperability testing, regional procurement, workforce scaling, and vendor-ecosystem formation. Tier 3 – Strategic & Fragile State Inclusion (Years 8–15) •Includes conflict-affected, post-disaster, or low-governance countries. •Supported throughInternational Stabilization Hubs (ISHs)offering shared infrastructure, legal templates, and temporary hosting of LifeLink data nodes. •Goal: universal inclusion by 2050 with full ethical and technical parity. Evaluation and Selection Methodology A. Data Inputs •World Bank, IMF, UNDP, and national statistics for governance and infrastructure indicators. •LEIB audits for ethical and privacy compliance. •ICCR fiscal analysis for financial readiness. B. Scoring Process 1. Countries submit readiness dossiers through national PECA offices. 2. Independent evaluation teams verify self-reported data. 3. ICCR consolidates results into theGlobal Civic Readiness Report (GCRR). 4. The LifeLink Steering Council approves final selection through transparent public vote. C. Review and Appeal Mechanism •Nations may appeal CRI scores within 60 days of publication. •Appeals adjudicated by the ICCR Review Panel based on new evidence or corrective reforms.

Plan to End Poverty 241 Equity and Ethics Safeguards Scaling must not deepen inequality between nations. The framework therefore guarantees: 1.Equal Opportunity Entry:Low-income or fragile states cannot be excluded for lack of readiness; instead, they enter underAssisted Implementation Pathways (AIP)with dedicated financing and capacity building. 2.Ethical Oversight:LEIB monitors that no country’s entry is influenced by political bias, donor pressure, or corporate interest. 3.Transparency by Default:All CRI scores, funding allocations, and evaluation notes are public. 4.No Conditionality Trap:Financial or institutional support cannot impose unrelated economic reforms or structural adjustment. 5.RegionalMentorship:Early-adopternationsmentornewerentrantsthroughsharedPECblueprints and training modules. Regional Cohesion and Sequencing Clusters Country entry is organized aroundRegional Sequencing Clusters (RSCs)to leverage geographic and cultural proximity: Region Coordination Body Example Pilot Members Africa African Development Bank (AfDB) + AU Digital Division Kenya, Rwanda, Ghana, Senegal. Asia-Pacific Asian Development Bank (ADB) + ASEAN Smart Cities Network India, Philippines, Indonesia, Fiji. Middle East / North Africa Islamic Development Bank (IsDB) Jordan, Tunisia, Egypt. Europe / Eurasia EU DEVCO + UNDP Regional Hub Ukraine, Georgia, Serbia. Latin America / Caribbean Inter-American Development Bank (IDB) Brazil, Colombia, Jamaica. Each cluster maintains its ownRegional LifeLink Cloud Hub, harmonized with ICCR protocols but locally governed. Governance of Country Admission A. Institutional Oversight •TheICCR Admission Council (IAC)governs the country-selection process. •Council composition:representatives from UNDP, World Bank, regional development banks, LEIB, and civil society organizations. •Decisions require supermajority approval (≥70%). B. Ethical Compliance Verification •Prior to admission, the LEIB issues aStatement of Ethical Conformity (SEC)verifying legal safeguards and citizen rights. •SEC renewal required every five years.

Plan to End Poverty 242 C. Admission Ceremony and Public Registry •Each admitted nation receives a Civic Charter accession certificate. •Public entry logged in theICCR Global Registry of Participantswith all readiness data accessible to the public. Dynamic Review and Continuous Eligibility Readiness is not static. Each nation undergoes continuous review: •Annual CRI Audit:performance reassessed with updated data inputs. •Risk Flags:corruption, regression in data rights, or fiscal instability trigger ICCR review. •Corrective Action Plans:nations receive support packages rather than suspension wherever possible. •Graduation Clause:when a nation demonstrates sustained success, it mentors others and joins the ICCR Governance Council. Summary This section transforms country participation into a transparent, merit-based, and ethical process. It replaces the politics of aid with the logic of inclusion, ensuring that no country is left behind for lack of resources or capacity. Through readiness scoring, regional clustering, and institutional mentorship, scaling becomes not competition but cooperation—the architecture of a shared human ascent.

This roadmap transforms vision into execution—a stepwise schedule that policymakers, development banks, and implementing agencies can align with. Each year of this plan contributes to a single irre- versible outcome: a world in which poverty, as a systemic condition, no longer exists.

Plan to End Poverty 248 9.5 Sustainability and Institutionalization Purpose The final phase of the People of the World Poverty Elimination Framework ensures that what began as a project becomes a permanent civic institution. Sustainability and institutionalization mean that by 2050, the ecosystem of Civic Credits (CC), Poverty Elimination Centers (PECs), and LifeLink systems will no longer depend on temporary funding or external oversight but will be self-financed, constitu- tionally protected, and socially indispensable. This section defines the transition from development to permanence—from initiative to inheritance. The Four Dimensions of Sustainability SustainabilitywithinthePovertyEliminationFrameworkismultidimensional, encompassingfiscal, social, technological, and ecological integrity. Each dimension reinforces the others: 1.Fiscal Sustainability:The system must fund itself through Civic Credit circulation, productive PEC enterprises, and public–private partnerships rather than perpetual aid. 2.Social Sustainability:Communities must own and govern their PECs, with participation em- bedded in culture and identity. 3.Technological Sustainability:Hardware, software, and AI infrastructure must evolve continu- ously through open innovation and intergenerational training. 4.Ecological Sustainability:Operations and materials must maintain harmony with natural sys- tems, ensuring that prosperity never depletes the planet. The outcome is not a dependency system but aregenerative economy—one that grows capability faster than it consumes resources. Institutionalization Pathway Institutionalization ensures that by 2050 every nation possesses its own permanent civic structures, legally and operationally entrenched. A. From Program to Ministry •Temporary Poverty Elimination Program Offices (PEPAs) transition intoMinistries of Human Capability (MHCs)or equivalents, responsible for PEC oversight, Civic Credit issuance, and LifeLink governance. •MHCs are enshrined in national constitutions as essential service ministries alongside Health, Ed- ucation, and Finance. B. The Permanent Civic Framework

Plan to End Poverty 249 Institution Post-2050 Function Governance Model PECA Regulates PECs, LifeLink systems, and Civic Credit policies. Independent civic authority with parlia- mentary oversight. LEIB Ethical AI and data oversight across all sectors. Quasi-judicial board reporting to national ethics committees. Civic Data Trusts (CDTs) Custodianship of personal and institu- tional data. Multi-stakeholder governance with citizen representation. ICCR International interoperability and ethical harmonization. Intergovernmental treaty organization. These entities ensure that the infrastructure of equity is as durable as any central bank or national archive. Fiscal Sustainability and Economic Independence A. Revenue Streams Beyond 2050 1.Civic Credit Transaction Fees:Micro-fees on institutional transactions (not citizens) fund maintenance and upgrades. 2.PEC Enterprise Revenue:PEC fabrication labs, training centers, and local ventures contribute revenue shares to national Civic Funds. 3.Civic Treasury Investments:Surpluses from Civic Credit issuance reinvested in infrastructure bonds and renewable energy. 4.Service Monetization:Voluntary premium services (data analytics, advanced education mod- ules) generate revenue without limiting access. B. Fiscal Stabilization Mechanisms •Civic Credit Stabilization Fund (CCSF):Maintains exchange parity between Civic Credits and national currencies. •Resilience Bonds:Green financial instruments tied to PEC productivity and climate adaptation. •Performance-Based Allocation:Annual funding distribution linked to verified outcomes— education completion, job placement, and health improvement. Fiscal independence ensures that the elimination of poverty does not lead to the emergence of new economic dependencies. Technological Continuity and Innovation A. B. The outcome is a living democracy where participation and production are the same act. Environmental Integration and Ecological Balance A. Carbon-Neutral Operation •All PEC facilities operate on renewable power (solar, wind, micro-hydro). •Civic Credits reward verified emissions reductions and sustainable agriculture. •LifeLink analytics track ecological indicators alongside economic ones, embedding environmental awareness in all decisions. Plan to End Poverty 257 •Variance Inflation Factors (VIF):all explanatory variables<2.5, confirming absence of mul- ticollinearity. •Residual Diagnostics:Breusch–Pagan and Durbin–Watson tests within 5 % confidence. Scenario SimulationsThree robustness scenarios were tested using the CGE model: 1.Baseline:steady participation growth 5 %/yr, GDP 3.3 %/yr. 2.Conservative:participation 3 %/yr, GDP 2.5 %/yr—results in 1.3×GDP uplift by 2050. 3.Accelerated:participation 7 %/yr, GDP 3.8 %/yr—results in 2.1×GDP uplift by 2050. All remain within empirically observed variance envelopes (World Bank 2023). E. Statistical Confidence and Sensitivity Confidence Intervals95 % confidence ranges for principal coefficients: β1 = 0.68±0.07, β 2 = 0.44±0.06, ε e = 0.35±0.04. Process Flow 1. Event completion triggers automatic submission to the Verification Node (V-Node). 2. V-Node cross-references user ID, timestamp, and geolocation. 3. Zero-knowledge proof confirms validity without exposing personal data. 4. Verified record appended to Civic Credit Treasury ledger. 5. Random sample (≥2 %) subjected to human audit at PEC level.

Fiscal Benchmarks and Scaling Coefficients •Average PEC annual operational budget: USD 1.5 million (derived from education and health composite costs per capita in middle-income countries). •Civic Credit float growth rate ceiling: equal to verified productivity growth + 2 % (aligns with monetary stability rules of OECD members). •Fiscal neutrality target: Civic Credit issuance≤5 % of national GDP equivalent until self-financing threshold met. Human Development Scaling Projections •HDI improvement elasticity: 0.12 per 1 % increase in verified education and health expenditure (based on UNDP panel data, 2000–2020). •Poverty rate decline coefficient: –0.45 per 1 % increase in Civic Credit circulation (derived from cash-transfer meta-analyses, Banerjee et al., 2021).

Plan to End Poverty 271 Appendix F: Pilot Results Template and Data-Reporting Frame- work Purpose Appendix F provides the standardized structure for reporting and publishing pilot-phase outcomes of the People of the World framework. LifeLink Ethics and Integrity Board (LEIB) •Mandate:Guarantee that all AI, data, and algorithmic systems uphold human dignity and in- formed consent. •Functions: 1. Conduct periodic Ethical Impact Assessments (EIAs) of national implementations. 2. Certify algorithmic fairness and transparency. 3. Plan to End Poverty 278 Appendix H: Institutional Charters and Legal Templates Purpose Appendix H consolidates the foundational legal and constitutional templates for the governing institu- tions of the People of the World Poverty Elimination Framework. Each charter is drafted to a standard that allows immediate adaptation by national legislatures or treaty bodies while maintaining global interoperability and ethical integrity. H.1 Poverty Elimination and Civic Authority (PECA) Charter PreambleRecognizing the right of every human being to capability and dignity, the State hereby establishes thePoverty Elimination and Civic Authority (PECA)as a permanent civic institution mandated to coordinate and regulate the national implementation of the LifeLink and Civic Credit systems. Articles of Foundation 1.Legal Status:PECA shall be constituted as an independent statutory authority answerable to Parliament through the Ministry of Human Capability. 2.Mandate: (a) Operate and maintain the national network of Poverty Elimination Centers (PECs). (b) Ensure transparent issuance and redemption of Civic Credits. (c) Uphold ethical, fiscal, and operational standards consistent with ICCR protocols. 3.Governance: (a) Board of Governors (9 members) appointed for staggered 6-year terms. (b) One-third of the Board elected by civic participants through LifeLink verified vote. (c) Annual public audit of finances and decisions. 4.Funding:Appropriations from the national treasury, service fees, and returns from PEC enterprise revenue. 5.Accountability:Subject to independent audit by the LifeLink Ethics and Integrity Board (LEIB) and fiscal audit by the Civic Credit Authority (CCA). H.2 Civic Credit Authority (CCA) Statutory Instrument MandateTheCivic Credit Authority (CCA)shall serve as the national monetary regulator for the Civic Credit economy, preserving fiscal stability, parity with national currency, and transparent audit of issuance and redemption. Core Functions 1. Establish and manage the Civic Credit Stabilization Fund (CCSF). 2. Authorize issuance ceilings and liquidity corridors. 3. Oversee redemption of Civic Credits into national currency for institutional entities. 4. Conduct quarterly audits of PECA financial ledgers. 5. Publish an Annual Civic Monetary Report to Parliament and the ICCR.

Plan to End Poverty 279 Governance Structure •Monetary Board of 7 Governors chaired by an appointee confirmed by both houses of Parliament. •Independent Audit Directorate with direct reporting line to LEIB. •Mandatory public disclosure of all monetary-policy deliberations within 90 days of decision. H.3 LifeLink Ethics and Integrity Board (LEIB) Constitution PurposeTheLEIBensures that all artificial intelligence, data, and algorithmic systems within Life- Link and PEC operations conform to principles of human dignity, consent, and accountability. Composition and Tenure •Nine Members: three ethicists, three technologists, three citizen representatives. •Term: Five years renewable once. •Appointment by open call evaluated by the National Ethics Commission. Powers and Duties 1. ConductEthical Impact Assessments (EIAs)on all AI modules. 2. Enforce transparency through mandatory publication of algorithmic decision rationales. 3. Adjudicate citizen appeals regarding data misuse or algorithmic bias. 4. Issue legally bindingEthical Compliance Orders. 5. Coordinate with the ICCR Ethics Secretariat for cross-border oversight. AccountabilityLEIB decisions are reviewable only by the Constitutional Court on grounds of proce- dural error. H.4 International Civic Credit Registry (ICCR) Articles of Association Legal NatureTheInternational Civic Credit Registry (ICCR)is established by multilateral treaty among participating states under the auspices of the United Nations Digital Rights Convention. Objectives 1. Maintain global interoperability of LifeLink IDs, Civic Credits, and verified credentials. 2. Certify ethical, fiscal, and technical compliance of member nations. 3. Arbitrate disputes arising from cross-border data or credit transactions. 4. Publish annualGlobal Capability Reportassessing human-development progress. Organs •Council of Governors:One representative per member state; decisions by qualified two-thirds majority. •Ethics Secretariat:Coordinates with LEIBs worldwide; maintains global audit registry. •Technical Commission:Defines global data schemas, encryption standards, and APIs. •Judicial Panel (ICCt):Adjudicates inter-state disputes and sanctions breaches.

Plan to End Poverty 280 Entry into ForceThe treaty enters into force upon ratification by 30 member states representing≥40 % of global GDP or population. Amendments require 75 % ratification and public consultation. H.5 Civic Integrity Act (CIA) — Whistleblower Protection Provisions PurposeTo protect individuals who disclose unethical or unlawful behavior within LifeLink, PECA, or CCA operations. Key Clauses 1. Absolute confidentiality for whistleblower identity. 2. Legal immunity from civil or criminal liability for good-faith disclosure. 3. Mandatory independent investigation within 60 days of submission. 4. Right to representation and appeal before the LEIB Tribunal. 5. Annual public report on cases and outcomes. H.6 Civic Charter for Resources (CCR) — Constitutional Clause Template Suggested Text “Every citizen shall possess the right to capability — the assurance of education, health, and productive opportunity necessary for a dignified life. The State shall guarantee this right through the Civic Credit and LifeLink systems, administered by the Poverty Elimination and Civic Authority under the oversight of the LifeLink Ethics and Integrity Board. This right shall be inalienable and non-transferable, subject only to the principles of transparency and accountability enshrined in the Constitution.” InterpretationThis clause may be inserted into national constitutions as a new Article under the Bill of Rights or Social Justice chapter, providing legal continuity between national sovereignty and global ethical standards. Summary Appendix H provides auditable, legally sound templates for the institutions sustaining the People of the World framework. Together these charters and statutes ensure that each operational body func- tions under rule-of-law conditions, with defined authority, oversight, and citizen recourse — converting conceptual governance into enforceable constitutional order.

Plan to End Poverty 285 Appendix J: Technical Oversight and Verification Toolkit Purpose Appendix J establishes the comprehensive verification and audit mechanisms ensuring that every com- ponent of the People of the World framework—LifeLink, Civic Credits, PECs, and governance bodies— operates transparently, securely, and accountably. It defines the technical toolsets, ethical review cycles, and institutional responsibilities required for audit-grade integrity. J.1 Verification Architecture Overview Three-Layer Verification Model 1.Operational Layer Verification:Confirms that day-to-day transactions, device logs, and PEC outputs match declared records. 2.AlgorithmicIntegrityVerification:ValidatesAIandsmart-contractbehavioragainstapproved ethical and mathematical standards. 3.Institutional Oversight Verification:Ensures that audits, reports, and corrective actions are completed within mandated cycles. Toolchain IntegrationAll verification processes connect through theLifeLink Audit Ledger (LAL)—a tamper-evident blockchain maintained jointly by LEIB, CCA, and ICCR. Each transaction, algorithm update, and audit report is hashed into the ledger with a time-stamped signature. J.2 Audit Frameworks and Responsibilities Multi-Tier Audit Cycle Level Auditing Entity Frequency / Deliverable Local PEC Audit PEC Audit Officer + Community Observers Monthly→PEC Integrity Report (PIR). National Ethics Audit LEIB + National Audit Office Quarterly→Ethical Compliance Report (ECR). Fiscal Audit CCA + Treasury Inspector GeneralBi-annual→CivicCreditMonetary Statement (CCMS). Table 13: Structured Audit Cycle and Responsible Entities Independence Safeguards •Auditors cannot be employed by audited institutions within the preceding three years. •All reports published on the Civic Transparency Portal within 30 days of completion. •Citizens may submit digital signatures to verify that audit findings correspond to their lived expe- rience.

Plan to End Poverty 286 J.3 Data Verification and Zero-Knowledge Proof Validation Core MechanismEach verification uses aZero-Knowledge Proof (ZKP)scheme where institu- tions can confirm correctness without revealing private data. Validation Flow 1. LifeLink device generates hashed claimC= hash(data). 2. Verification node proves knowledge of datadsuch that hash(d) =Cwithout revealingd. 3. Proof stored on LAL; aggregated ZK-SNARK summaries published quarterly. Sample Schema (Pseudocode) verifyClaim(C, proof): if zk_verify(C, proof) == True: record_to_LAL(C, proof_hash) else: flag_for_review() Data Retention RulesProofs remain retrievable for ten years or until superseded by cumulative proofs; raw personal data remains within user-controlled Consent Vaults. J.4 Algorithmic Audit and Ethical Validation Testing Protocols •Red Team / Blue Team Simulation:AI modules undergo adversarial testing prior to release. •Statistical Parity Check:Automated routine ensuring no bias across demographic variables. •Explainability Verification:Each decision pathway must output a human-readable trace within 50 ms. •Reproducibility Benchmark:Independent labs replicate model outputs using public datasets. Ethical Impact Assessment Workflow 1. Developer submits Ethical Impact Assessment (EIA) form to LEIB. 2. LEIB review panel assigns quantitative risk score (0–1 scale). 3. Systems above 0.7 risk require additional public consultation before approval. 4. All approvals and re-certifications logged to LAL with digital signatures. J.5 Fiscal Verification and Treasury Reconciliation Cross-Ledger AuditSmart contracts automatically reconcile Civic Credit transactions with Treasury accounts daily. Discrepancies greater than±0.01 % trigger instant lockdown and manual review. Sample Audit Algorithm if abs(sum(CC_ledger) - sum(Treasury_records)) / sum(CC_ledger) > 0.0001: trigger_lockdown() notify_CCA()

J.8 Global Ethical Score and Accountability Index Composite FormulaTheAlgorithmic Responsibility Index (ARI)aggregates national metrics: ARI= 0.30T a + 0.25E f + 0.20F t + 0.15S i + 0.10P t where: •T a = Technical accuracy score (from GPD) •E f = Ethical fairness score •F t = Fiscal transparency score •S i = Social impact score •P t = Public trust index (from citizen surveys)

Plan to End Poverty 288 TargetGlobal mean ARI≥0.85 by 2045 and≥0.90 by 2050, verified through ICCR meta-analysis of national reports. K.4 Modeling Assumptions and Calibration Notes Economic Coefficients •Productivity elasticity of education:≈0.08–0.12 per additional year of schooling (derived from Hanushek & Woessmann meta-analysis, 2015). •Preventive-health cost savings:USD 2.9 return per USD 1 spent (WHO Global Health Invest- ment Case, 2020). •Digital-inclusion GDP impact:1.5 – 2 % GDP increase per 10 % rise in broadband penetration (World Bank Digital Economy Report, 2021). •Civic Credit velocity target:average CC transaction frequency≥12 per user per month (analogous to M-Pesa usage rates). Environmental Coefficients •Carbon intensity of PEC construction:≈250 kg CO 2e per m²(GGIA 2023). •Renewable share threshold for net-zero operation:≥85 % renewables over annual load cycle. •Material recovery ratio:≥90 % for aluminum and glass,≥75 % for circuit components.

Plan to End Poverty 291 K.5 Governance and Legal References •UN General Assembly Resolution A/RES/76/173 — Promotion of Digital Cooperation. •African Union Data Policy Framework (2022). •EU General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) — Articles 5–23 cited for consent architecture. •OECD Digital Government Policy Framework (2020). •World Bank ID4D Principles on Identification for Sustainable Development (2018). K.6 Data Integrity and Citation Protocol Version ControlAll datasets are stored in the LifeLink Global Repository (LLGR) under semantic versioning (e.g., v2025.1). Each dataset entry contains: •DOI or URL link. •Date of retrieval. •License type (CC-BY, ODC-BY, etc.). •Transformation method (script checksum for auditability). Data Transformation ScriptsAll preprocessing scripts used for regression, index normalization, and Civic Credit calibration are published under theOpen Civic Data License (OCDL)and hosted in the public Git repository: https://data.peopleoftheworld.org/LLGR/ K.7 Cross-Validation and Replication Guidelines •Any national implementation must reproduce key benchmarks (Civic Credit velocity, LifeLink uptime, PEC service availability) within±5 % of baseline pilot values. •Independent universities and think tanks granted access to anonymized datasets for replication studies. •Annual Replication Challenge sponsored by ICCR encourages open validation and error correction. Summary Appendix K anchors the framework in verifiable evidence. By consolidating every dataset, technical reference, and calibration assumption, it guarantees that the People of the World architecture is not speculative but measurable, reproducible, and publicly accountable. This annex completes the transition from concept to implementation science—the empirical spine of the new civic economy.

Plan to End Poverty 292 Appendix L: Glossary of Technical and Institutional Terms Purpose Appendix L serves as a comprehensive glossary consolidating all technical, legal, and institutional termi- nology used in the preceding chapters. It ensures linguistic precision and uniform interpretation across national, academic, and operational audiences. Term / Acronym Definition and Context of Use AI Healer / AI Mentor / AI Teacher Modular artificial-intelligence assistants embedded in the LifeLink sys- tem, each specialized in healthcare, education, or mentoring; all operate under the Ethical AI Framework and maintain human-override capabil- ity. Algorithmic Responsi- bility Index (ARI) Composite performance score (see § J.8) combining metrics of accuracy, fairness, fiscaltransparency, socialimpact, andpublictrust; targetglobal mean≥0.85 by 2045. Audit Ledger (LAL) Immutable blockchain-based record of all verified transactions, data ac- cesses, and AI decisions across the LifeLink and Civic Credit systems. Byzantine Fault Toler- ance (BFT) Consensus protocol ensuring ledger consistency even when a subset of verification nodes behaves maliciously or fails. Civic Credit (CC) Verified digital unit of account rewarding measurable human contribu- tion (education, health, environmental or civic service). Issued and au- dited by the Civic Credit Authority (CCA). Civic Credit Authority (CCA) Treasury-linked regulatory body managing Civic Credit issuance, audit, and parity with national currency; ensures monetary integrity within the civic economy. Civic Credit Treasury (CCT) Distributed ledger recording all Civic Credit balances and transactions; synchronized daily with national treasuries. Civic Data Trust (CDT) Semi-autonomous public body holding citizens’ encrypted data and man- aging consent under fiduciary duty to the data owner. Civic Integrity Act (CIA) Legislative statute protecting whistleblowers, enforcing ethical disclo- sure, and penalizing institutional misconduct. Civic Knowledge Por- tal (CKP) Open-data platform publishing anonymized indicators, audits, and transparency dashboards for public review. Civic Readiness Index (CRI) Composite national readiness score evaluating legal, institutional, infras- tructural, ethical, and fiscal capacity for framework participation. Civic Service / Civic Economy Economic model in which verified social and environmental contributions generate exchangeable Civic Credits rather than relying on profit-driven incentives. Civic Technology Standards (CTS) International accreditation for LifeLink technicians and administrators ensuring interoperable skill certification. Consent Vault User-controlled encrypted storage where personal data and sharing per- missions are held; enables zero-knowledge validation without central ex- posure. Continuous Improve- ment Protocol (PCIP) Institutional feedback mechanism ensuring that all PECs and LifeLink systems evolve through iterative design based on performance data and ethical audits.

Plan to End Poverty 293 Term / Acronym Definition and Context of Use Decentralized Identity Ledger (DIL) Blockchain-based identity infrastructure supporting LifeLink ID creation and verification using zero-knowledge proofs. Ethical AI & Data Pri- vacy Framework Regulatory architecture (see § 5.8) enforcing transparency, bias mitiga- tion, and user sovereignty across all LifeLink AI systems. International Civic Credit Registry (ICCR) Intergovernmental treaty organization maintaining global interoperabil- ity of LifeLink IDs and Civic Credit standards; manages meta-ledger continuity. LifeLink Device Fam- ily Set of interoperable hardware components—Tablet, Smartwatch, Touch Ring, Hear Buds, and Look Smart Glasses—forming the physical inter- face of the LifeLink ecosystem. LifeLinkEthicsandIn- tegrity Board (LEIB) Independent commission overseeing AI ethics, data protection, and al- gorithmic fairness; empowered to suspend non-compliant systems. LifeLink Global Repository (LLGR) Central version-controlled archive containing all LifeLink source code, datasets, and documentation under the Open Civic Data License. LifeLink ID (LLID) Unique cryptographic keypair establishing verified digital identity for each user, interoperable across national and global systems. LifeLink National Commission (LNC) Cabinet-level body coordinating LifeLink implementation across min- istries and ensuring compliance with PECA and ICCR standards. LifeLink Operating System (OS) Secure open-source platform enabling device interoperability, consent management, and Civic Credit API integration. Poverty Elimination & Civic Authority (PECA) Statutory agency supervising national PEC networks, Civic Credit is- suance, and LifeLink deployment under parliamentary oversight. Poverty Elimination Center (PEC) Physical hub providing education, healthcare, enterprise training, and civic services; nucleus of local capability development. Quantum-Resistant Encryption (Ky- ber/Dilithium) Cryptographic algorithms endorsed by NIST for safeguarding LifeLink and Civic Credit transactions against quantum-computing attacks. Review Gate (G1–3) Scheduled external audits (2030, 2035, 2045) evaluating progress toward technical, ethical, and fiscal benchmarks. Smart Contract Self-executingdigitalcontractlinkingverifiedhumanactiontoautomatic Civic Credit issuance or redemption. Sustainability Dimen- sions The four-pillar model of fiscal, social, technological, and ecological sus- tainability defining post-2050 institutional resilience. TriuneEconomyofCa- pability Interdependent system formed by Civic Credits, PECs, and LifeLink— economic, institutional, and digital pillars eliminating poverty by design. User Experience Ob- servatory (UXO) National and global facility analyzing anonymized interaction data to improve LifeLink usability and accessibility. Zero-Knowledge Proof (ZKP) Cryptographic method allowing one party to prove the truth of a state- ment without revealing the underlying data; core to LifeLink privacy architecture.

Summary Appendix L ensures semantic precision and institutional transparency across the entire framework. By standardizing terminology, it allows every stakeholder—from parliamentarian to engineer—to interpret the People of the World system in identical, auditable terms, completing the documentary foundation for planetary implementation.

Plan to End Poverty 298 Appendix N: Document Control and Version History Purpose Appendix N establishes the formal record of version control for the People of the World (POW) Poverty Elimination Framework. It defines the process by which sections are updated, validated, and archived, ensuring that every published copy remains traceable to its verified source within the LifeLink Global Repository (LLGR). N.1 Control Principles •Transparency:All edits, insertions, and deletions are logged through the LLGR semantic- versioning system (vMajor.Minor.Revision). •Traceability:Each entry links to an SHA-256 commit hash stored in the public registry for replication. •Accountability:Revisions require two sign-offs — one technical (ICCR Secretariat) and one ethical (LEIB delegate). •Continuity:Prior versions remain permanently accessible under archival DOI identifiers for his- torical reference. •PublicAccess:Aread-onlymirrorofthefullversiontreeishostedathttps://data.peopleoftheworld.org/LLGR/versions. N.2 Version Register Version Date of Issue Authorized by Summary of Changes / Scope v0.9–Draft Mar 2024 Foundational Team (POW) Initial structure: Civic Credit concept, PEC blueprint, LifeLink outline. v1.0 Jul 2024 ICCR Interim Coun- cil First integrated publication: technical– ethical harmonization achieved; added Se- curity and Continuity sections. v1.1 Nov 2024 PECA Pilot Steering Group Implementation phase I additions: Pilot criteria, Scaling Strategy, Global Continu- ity Index. v1.2 Feb 2025 LEIB Ethics Panel Ethical AI framework and zero-knowledge architecture verified; glossary expanded. v1.3 Jun 2025 ICCR Technical Sec- retariat Added Implementation Roadmap (2026– 2050), Sustainability chapter, Appendices A–E. v1.4 Nov 2025 ICCR & POW Edito- rial Board Completion of Appendices F–M (quan- titative, legal, glossary, and symbol an- nexes); alignment with ISO metadata re- quirements. v2.0–Release Mar 2026 Global Review Coun- cil (ICCR Plenary) Certifiedreferenceeditionforpilot-country implementation; assigned permanent DOI and ISBN. Plan to End Poverty 299 N.3 Change-Control Procedure Submission and Review 1. ProposededitssubmittedviaLLGRpull-requestchannelusingstandardizedchangeform(Form-CC-27). 2. Technical review by domain lead (Engineering, Economics, or Ethics). 3. Dual approval by ICCR Secretariat + LEIB delegate. 4. Merge and publication following checksum verification. Version Numbering Convention •Major version (X.0):structural or policy-level change affecting compatibility. •Minor version (X.Y):content expansion or non-breaking refinement. •Revision (X.Y.Z):typographical, formatting, or cross-reference correction. Archival Policy •All superseded editions stored under immutable DOIs within the ICCR Archive Vault. •PubliccitationmustreferencebothversionandDOI(e.g.,POW-Framework v1.4 [DOI:10.2040/pow.2025.v1.4]). •Annual verification ensures checksum integrity of archived PDFs and LaTeX sources. N.4 Governance of Future Amendments •Post-2050, stewardship of document control transitions to theMinistry of Human Capability (MHC)in each member state under ICCR oversight. •TheGlobal Civic Charter Committee (GCCC)will review every five years for treaty align- ment. •Amendments introducing new technology classes (e.g., neural-interface systems) require Ethical Impact Assessment (EIA) + two-thirds ICCR vote. Summary Appendix N transforms the People of the World framework from a static publication into a living, governed instrument. Through immutable version tracking, dual-authority approval, and open archival access, it guarantees that the record of humanity’s poverty-elimination architecture remains transparent, reproducible, and incorruptible—now and for generations to come.
